<p>Worldwide <b>growth of photovoltaics</b> has been fitting an <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">exponential curve</a> for more than two decades. During this period of time, <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaics</a> (PV), also known as solar PV, has evolved from a pure niche market of small scale applications towards becoming a mainstream electricity source. When <a href="/wiki/Solar_PV_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar PV system">solar PV systems</a> were first recognized as a promising <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> technology, programs, such as <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariffs</a>, were implemented by a number of governments in order to provide economic incentives for investments. For several years, growth was mainly driven by Japan and pioneering European countries. As a consequence, cost of solar declined significantly due to improvements in technology and <a href="/wiki/Economies_of_scale" title="Economies of scale">economies of scale</a>, even more so when production of <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">modules</a> started to ramp up in China.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian-china_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-china-6">[6]</a></sup> Since then, deployment of photovoltaics is gaining momentum on a worldwide scale, particularly in Asia but also in North America and other regions, where solar PV is now increasingly competing with <a href="/wiki/Conventional_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Conventional energy">conventional energy</a> sources as <a href="/wiki/Grid_parity" title="Grid parity">grid parity</a> has already been reached in about 30 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-deutsche-2015-chasm_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deutsche-2015-chasm-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9</sup></p>
<p>Projections for photovoltaic growth are difficult and burdened with many uncertainties. Official agencies, such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> consistently increased their estimates over the years, but still fell short of actual deployment.<sup id="cite_ref-energywatchgroup_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energywatchgroup-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-energypost-iea_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energypost-iea-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-theenergycollective-iea_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theenergycollective-iea-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">United States</a> had been the leader of installed photovoltaics for many years, and its total <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">capacity</a> amounted to 77 <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">megawatts</a> in 1996—more than any other country in the world at the time. Then, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Japan" title="Solar power in Japan">Japan</a> stayed ahead as the world's leader of produced solar electricity until 2005, when <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Germany" title="Solar power in Germany">Germany</a> took the lead. The country is currently approaching the 40,000 megawatt mark. <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a> is expected to continue its rapid growth and to triple its PV capacity to 70,000 megawatts by 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg-News-CHN-70GW_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-News-CHN-70GW-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CleanTechnica_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CleanTechnica-12">[12]</a></sup> In 2015, China became world's largest producer of photovoltaic power.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> By the end of 2015, cumulative photovoltaic capacity reached at least 227 <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">gigawatts</a> (GW), sufficient to supply 1 percent of global electricity demands. Solar now contributes 8%, 7.4% percent and 7.1 percent to the respective annual domestic consumption in <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Italy" title="Solar power in Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Greece" title="Solar power in Greece">Greece</a> and Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2015_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2015-5">[5]</a></sup> For 2016, worldwide deployment of up to <a href="#Forecast">77&#160;GW is being forecasted</a>, and installed capacity is projected to more than double or even triple beyond 500&#160;GW between now and 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2015_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2015-2">[2]</a></sup> By 2050, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> is anticipated to become the world's largest source of electricity, with solar photovoltaics and <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a> contributing 16 and 11 percent, respectively. This will require PV capacity to grow to 4,600&#160;GW, of which more than half is forecasted to be deployed in China and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_India" title="Solar power in India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Current status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Current status describes worldwide, regional and domestic solar PV deployment as of the end of 2014 (<i>see section <a href="#Forecast">Forecast</a> for 2015</i>). The unit of power, <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watt</a>, is frequently used as multiples, such as <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt">kilowatt</a> (kW), <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">megawatt</a> (MW), <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> (GW) and <a href="/wiki/Terawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Terawatt">terawatt</a> (TW). <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">Nameplate capacity</a> in the article is displayed as MW and has to be understood as direct current mega<a href="/wiki/Watt-peak" class="mw-redirect" title="Watt-peak">watt-peak</a> (MW<sub>p</sub>), if not otherwise explicitly denoted as, for example, <a href="/wiki/Watt-peak_AC" class="mw-redirect" title="Watt-peak AC">MW<sub>AC</sub></a><sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:15</sup><sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2013_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2013-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:10</sup></p>
<p>For a complete history of deployment over the last two decades, also see section <i><a href="#History_of_deployment">History of deployment</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide">Worldwide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Worldwide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014, cumulative photovoltaic capacity increased by 40.1&#160;GW or 28% and reached at least 178&#160;GW by the end of the year, sufficient to supply 1 percent of the world's total <a href="/wiki/Electricity_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="Electricity consumption">electricity consumption</a> of currently 18,400&#160;<a href="/wiki/TWh" class="mw-redirect" title="TWh">TWh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2015_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5–6</sup> Although this represents a new all-time record in the <a href="#History_of_deployment">history of global PV deployment</a>, overall expectations had been higher as module shipments amounted to 44–46&#160;GW and suggested higher overall installations.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2015_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Annual installation for 2014 expanded slightly by 5% when compared to worldwide installation of 38.3&#160;GW in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9</sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions">Regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014, Asia was the fastest growing region, with more than 60% of global installations. China and Japan alone accounted for 20&#160;GW or half of worldwide deployment. Europe continued to decline and installed 7&#160;GW or 18% of the global PV market, three times less than in the record-year of 2011, when 22&#160;GW had been installed. For the first time, North and South America combined accounted for at least as much as Europe, about 7.1&#160;GW or about 18% of global total. This is due to the strong growth in the United States, supported by Canada, Chile and Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In terms of cumulative capacity, Europe is still the most developed region with 88&#160;GW or half of the global total of 178&#160;GW. Solar PV now covers 3.5% and 7% of European electricity demand and <a href="/wiki/Peak_electricity_demand" class="mw-redirect" title="Peak electricity demand">peak electricity demand</a>, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:6</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific" title="Asia-Pacific">Asia-Pacific</a> region (APAC) which includes countries such as Japan, India and Australia, follows second and accounts for about 20% percent of worldwide capacity. In third position ranks China with 16%, followed by the Americas with about 12%. Cumulative capacity in the MEA (Middle East and Africa) region and ROW (rest of the world) accounted for only about 3.3% of the global total. A great untapped potential remains for many of these countries, especially in the Sunbelt.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Countries">Countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #33CCFF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Chile" title="Solar power in Chile">Chile</a> (0.9%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #8C8C22; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Germany" title="Solar power in Germany">Germany</a> (4.9%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #B3CC33; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Italy" title="Solar power in Italy">Italy</a> (1.0%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #54C571; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Solar power in the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (5.9%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:forestgreen; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar power in the European Union">Rest of Europe</a> (5.6%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFF380; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_South_Africa" title="Solar power in South Africa">South Africa</a> (2.1%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #666666; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Rest of the World (1.4%)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As in the year before, the world's top installer of 2014 were <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a> (+10.6&#160;GW), followed by <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Japan" title="Solar power in Japan">Japan</a> (+9.6&#160;GW) and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">United States</a> (+6.2&#160;GW), while the <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Solar power in the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (+2.3&#160;GW) emerged as new European leader ahead of Germany (+1.9&#160;GW) and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_France" title="Solar power in France">France</a> (+0.9&#160;GW). Germany remains for one more year the world's largest producer of solar power with an overall installed capacity of 38.2&#160;GW.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Chile (+0.4&#160;GW) and South Africa (+0.8&#160;GW) were the newcomers of 2014. South Africa entered the top 10 in added capacity rankings for the first time. There are now twenty countries around the world with a cumulative PV capacity of more than one gigawatt <i>(see <a href="#Deployment_by_country">bar-chart</a> below)</i>. Thailand (1,299&#160;MW), The Netherlands (1,123&#160;MW), and Switzerland (1,076&#160;MW), all crossed the gigawatt threshold in 2014. Based on IEA's data, the available solar PV capacity in Italy, Germany and Greece is now sufficient to supply between 7% and 8% of their respective domestic electricity consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other mentionable PV deployments above the 100-megawatt mark included <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Canada" title="Solar power in Canada">Canada</a> (500&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Thailand" title="Solar power in Thailand">Thailand</a> (475&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_Netherlands" title="Solar power in the Netherlands">The Netherlands</a> (400&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Taiwan" title="Solar power in Taiwan">Taiwan</a> (400&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Italy" title="Solar power in Italy">Italy</a> (385&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Chile" title="Solar power in Chile">Chile</a> (1,113&#160;MW),<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Switzerland" title="Solar power in Switzerland">Switzerland</a> (320&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Israel" title="Solar power in Israel">Israel</a> (250&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Austria" title="Solar power in Austria">Austria</a> (140&#160;MW) and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Portugal" title="Solar power in Portugal">Portugal</a> (110&#160;MW).<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup> Underperforming countries were Belgium (65&#160;MW), Bulgaria (2&#160;MW), the Czech Republic (2&#160;MW), Greece (16&#160;MW), Romania (69&#160;MW), Slovakia (0.4&#160;MW) and Spain (22&#160;MW), with very low to almost non-existent additions compared to previous years.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forecast">Forecast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Forecast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forecast_for_2016">Forecast for 2016</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Forecast for 2016">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 2016, Mercom Capital Group, forecasted global solar installations to reach 66.7 GW with China, the United States, Japan and India to make up the top four solar markets in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-2016-04-26_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-2016-04-26-21">[21]</a></sup> However, in December 2016, Mercom increased their 2016 forecast to 76GW.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Market research firm IHS forecast in February global installations to reach 69 GW in 2016. Meanwhile, Greentech Media has a 2016 forecast of 64 GW.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In December 2016, IHS updated their 2016 forecast to 77GW.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forecast_for_2015">Forecast for 2015</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Forecast for 2015">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumbcaption">Projected global cumulative capacity in MW<br />
<p><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #CCA300; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ffcc00; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> historical cumulative capacity</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #CC0000; padding:1px .6em; background-color:red; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> average projection for 2015 (+55 GW, 233&#160;GW)</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #0092DB; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#29B8FF; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> low scenario reaches 396&#160;GW by 2019</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #40FF1A; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#7FFF66; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> high scenario reaches 540&#160;GW by 2019</span><br /></p>
Source: <a href="/wiki/European_Photovoltaic_Industry_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="European Photovoltaic Industry Association">SPE</a>, Global Market Outlook 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2015_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup> amended with industry average for 2015<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[a]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In June 2015, SolarPower Europe (SPE) – the former European Photovoltaic Industry Association (EPIA) – released its new report, <i>Global Market Outlook for Solar Power 2015–2019</i>. The European PV organization expects global installations to grow between 41&#160;GW and 60&#160;GW, marked by their low and high scenario, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[b]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-epia-2015_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup> A year before, the European lobby association estimated 2015 to grow by 35–53&#160;GW.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:39</sup></p>
<p>The International Energy Agency (IEA) will still have to update its forecast in the course of 2015. This is expected to happen on 1 October 2015, when the <i>Medium-Term Renewable Energy Market Report 2015</i> will be launched on the sidelines of the G20 summit in Istanbul, Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> In August 2014, IEA forecasted 38&#160;GW in its baseline scenario for 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-mtremr-2014-exec_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-mtremr-2014-exec-28">[26]</a></sup> The IEA has been criticized for systematically underestimating the growth of photovoltaics in the past.<sup id="cite_ref-energywatchgroup_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energywatchgroup-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>IHS Technology forecasts global solar PV installations to grow by 59&#160;GW or 33% in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-ihs-2015-10-13_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs-2015-10-13-29">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-solarserver-ihs_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solarserver-ihs-30">[28]</a></sup> The company also predicts an accelerated growth for <a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">concentrator photovoltaics</a>, an increase in market-share of <a href="/wiki/Monocrystalline_silicon" title="Monocrystalline silicon">monocrystalline silicon</a> technology over <a href="/wiki/Polycrystalline_silicon" title="Polycrystalline silicon">polycrystalline silicon</a>, currently the leading semiconductor material used for solar cells <i>(also see section <a href="#Technologies_.281990.E2.80.93present.29">Technologies</a>)</i>, and that <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_California" title="Solar power in California">solar power in California</a> will provide more than 10 percent of the state's annual power generation, higher than in Italy and Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-IHS-2015-top-trends_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-2015-top-trends-31">[29]</a></sup></p>

<p>Deutsche Bank (DB) anticipates deployment to reach about 54&#160;GW in 2015. An increase in investments and improvement of cost competitiveness is expected, while weaker oil prices are not seen to play a significant role for the solar sector.<sup id="cite_ref-deutsche-2015-forecast-feb15_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deutsche-2015-forecast-feb15-32">[30]</a></sup> They find that <a href="/wiki/Grid_parity" title="Grid parity">grid parity</a> has arrived in 30 countries around the world (compared to 19 markets the year before<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup>), as unsubsidized rooftop solar costs $0.08–$0.13 per kilowatt-hour, and is now below the retail prices of electricity in these markets. DB also estimates current installation cost to range from $1.00/W for utility-scale systems in China to $2.90/W for U.S. residential rooftop systems.<sup id="cite_ref-deutsche-2015-chasm_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deutsche-2015-chasm-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>For 2015, Mercom Capital (MC) predicts global installation to amount to 57.8&#160;GW<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-2015-06-17_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-2015-06-17-34">[32]</a></sup> (up from 54.5&#160;GW predicted a few months earlier<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-mar-2015_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-mar-2015-35">[33]</a></sup>), while Bloomberg New Energy Finance (BNEF) foresees solar PV to add more than 55&#160;GW. The <i>10 Predictions For Clean Energy In 2015</i> by Michael Liebreich mentions the spread of PV to more and more localities in Africa, the trend of imposing taxes on rooftop systems, and the growing confidence among investors that solar is indeed a cheap source of power.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNEF-NEO2015_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNEF-NEO2015-37">[35]</a></sup> In June 2015, Greentech Media (GTM) Research estimated global PV installations at 55&#160;GW for 2015 and notes that this corresponds to about half of the world's newly installed electricity generating capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-gtm-2015-06-17_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gtm-2015-06-17-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 40 countries are expected to install more than 100 megawatts in 2015 (compared to 25 countries in 2014).<sup id="cite_ref-IHS-2015-top-trends_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-2015-top-trends-31">[29]</a></sup> In the United States, installations are predicted to grow by 7.9&#160;GW (SEIA<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup>) to 9.4&#160;GW, up by about 30–45% over the record-year of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-2015-06-17_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-2015-06-17-34">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ihs-2015-03-30_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs-2015-03-30-40">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-US-Solar-Market-Insight-2014_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US-Solar-Market-Insight-2014-41">[39]</a></sup> Both, the United Kingdom (2.9–3.5&#160;GW) and Japan (9–10.4&#160;GW) are being forecasted to set new records in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-2015-06-17_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-2015-06-17-34">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ihs-2015-03-30_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs-2015-03-30-40">[38]</a></sup> After three years of decline, installations in Europe are expected to grow again to 9.4&#160;GW, up 19% over 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-solarserver-ihs_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solarserver-ihs-30">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IHS-2015-estimate2_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-2015-estimate2-42">[40]</a></sup> The Chinese government set its own 2015 solar target to 17.8&#160;GW, much higher than its original 2014 target it ultimately missed to achieve.<sup id="cite_ref-CleanTechnica_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CleanTechnica-12">[12]</a></sup> India is expected to install more than 2&#160;GW, a tripling over the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-may-2015_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-may-2015-43">[41]</a></sup> A return of deployment in the gigawatt-scale is predicted for France, and record installations of 1.1&#160;GW are expected for Thailand, while deployment in Australia and Germany would remain unchanged.<sup id="cite_ref-IHS-2015-estimate2_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-2015-estimate2-42">[40]</a></sup> Latin America is forecasted to install 2.2&#160;GW in 2015, with a significant contribution from the Central American region for the first time, while Chile and Mexico are expected to double and triple their installations, respectively. The projected top five Latin American installers of 2015 are Chile (1&#160;GW), Honduras (460&#160;MW), Mexico (195&#160;MW), Guatemala (98&#160;MW) and Panama (62&#160;MW).<sup id="cite_ref-gtm-latinameric-2015_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gtm-latinameric-2015-44">[42]</a></sup> Rapid growth of solar PV is also expected to occur in <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Jordan" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar power in Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Pakistan" title="Solar power in Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_the_Philippines" title="Energy in the Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IHS-2015-top-trends_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-2015-top-trends-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup></p>

&#160;<sup>7</sup> Difference to global cumulative as of the end of 2014 (178&#160;GW) <sup id="cite_ref-epia-2015_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup></th>
</tr>
</table>
<p>There are a number of short and medium term forecasts published by several organizations and market research companies. In addition, the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA) and <a href="/wiki/European_Photovoltaic_Industry_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="European Photovoltaic Industry Association">Solar Power Europe</a> (SPE, the former EPIA) produce more than one scenario each. The summary table shows the different forecasts for global PV capacity by 2020. Projections are listed by ascending cumulative capacity. The table also shows the capacity that has to be installed from 2015–2020 and the average annual installation required to meet the projection. Conservative scenarios forecast capacity to reach 400 or more gigawatts, assuming declining annual installations from current levels, while more optimistic scenarios project cumulative capacity to grow beyond 500&#160;GW. Only the most optimistic projections around 600&#160;GW foresee annual installations to grow above 10 percent (p.a) in the near future.</p>
<p>The European Photovoltaic Industry Association expects the fastest PV growth to continue in China, South-East Asia, Latin America, the Middle-East, North Africa, and India. By 2019, worldwide capacity is projected to reach between 396&#160;GW (low scenario) and 540&#160;GW (high scenario). This corresponds to a more than doubling and tripling of installed capacity within five years, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2015_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:15</sup></p>
<p>Consulting firm <a href="/wiki/Frost_%26_Sullivan" title="Frost &amp; Sullivan">Frost &amp; Sullivan</a> projects global PV capacity to increase to 446&#160;GW by 2020, with China, India and North America being the fastest growing regions, while Europe is expected to double its solar capacity from current levels.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> Grand View Research, a market research and consulting firm, headquartered in San Francisco, published its solar PV forecast report in March 2015. The large PV potential in countries such as Brazil, Chile and Saudi Arabia has not expanded as expected and is supposed to be explored over the next six years. In addition, China's increase of manufacturing capacity is expected to further lowering global market prices. The consulting firm projects worldwide cumulative deployment to reach about 490&#160;GW by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>PV Market Alliance</i> (PVMA), a recently founded consortium of several research bodies, sees global PV capacity to reach 444–630&#160;GW by 2020. In its low scenario, annual installations are projected to grow from 40 to 50 gigawatts by the end of the decade, while its high scenario forecasts deployment to increase from 60 to 90&#160;GW during the next five years. The medium scenario therefore expects annual PV installations to grow from 50&#160;GW to 70&#160;GW and to reach 536&#160;GW by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-pvma-brochure-2015_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pvma-brochure-2015-46">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> PVMA's figures are in line with those published earlier by Solar Power Europe. In June 2015, Greentech Media (GTM) Research released its <i>Global PV Demand Outlook</i> for 2020. The company projects annual installations to increase from 40&#160;GW to 135&#160;GW and global cumulative capacity to reach almost 700&#160;GW by 2020. GTM's outlook is the most aggressive of all forecasts to date, with projected deployment of 518&#160;GW between 2015 and 2020, or more than twice as much as IEA's 225&#160;GW baseline case scenario, published ten months earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-gtm-outlook-2015-2020_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gtm-outlook-2015-2020-52">[50]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable floatleft" style="text-align: center; font-size: 0.9em; margin-right: 12px;">

</tr>
<tr>
<th colspan="5" style="font-size: 0.92em; text-align: left; padding: 6px 2px; font-weight: normal;">Source: <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">IEA</a> <i>Medium Term Renewable Energy Market</i><br />
data from 2013-edition,<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-mtremr-2013-exec_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-mtremr-2013-exec-53">[51]</a></sup> 2014-edition<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-mtremr-2014-exec_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-mtremr-2014-exec-28">[26]</a></sup> and 2015-edition<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup></th>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The International Energy Agency (IEA) sees overall stagnating annual installations in the range of 36–39&#160;GW until 2020, when global capacity will reach 403&#160;GW, according to the highlighted baseline case scenario of the <i>Medium Term Renewable Energy Market 2014</i> report.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-mtremr-2014-exec_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-mtremr-2014-exec-28">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IEA-mtremr-2014_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-mtremr-2014-55">[53]</a></sup> Paradoxically, since the report's 2013-edition, projected cumulative for 2018 has increased by 6% from 308&#160;GW to 326&#160;GW, while the corresponding annual deployment decreased.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-mtremr-2013-exec_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-mtremr-2013-exec-53">[51]</a></sup> This is due to the fact that the International Energy Agency adjusted annual installations upward on the near-end – in order to meet actual deployment, while reducing estimates on the far-end. The result is a flat curve that stays below 40&#160;GW until 2020 <i>(see table)</i>. For 2017, the projected low of 36&#160;GW concurs with the scheduled expiration of the solar <a href="/wiki/Investment_tax_credit" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment tax credit">investment tax credit</a> (ITC) in the U.S. and the expected end of the solar boom in Japan. IEA's projected annual installation of less than 40&#160;GW also leads to a negative growth rate, since expectations for 2015 are much higher <i>(see <a href="#Forecast_for_2015">Forecast for 2015</a>)</i>. Such a decline, however, is unprecedented and has never been observed in the recorded history of solar PV deployment. This scenario makes IEA's baseline case the most conservative of all projections. In the less featured <i>enhanced high case scenario</i>, IEA estimates that "solar PV could reach a cumulative 465&#160;GW to 515&#160;GW in 2020"<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-mtremr-2014-exec_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-mtremr-2014-exec-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> and that "solar PV capacity could top 500 GW globally in 2020".<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-mtremr-2014_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-mtremr-2014-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2020, IEA's <i>Technology Roadmap: Solar Photovoltaic Energy report</i> expects China to account for over 110&#160;GW of solar PV, while Japan and Germany would each reach around 50&#160;GW. The United States would rank fourth at over 40&#160;GW, followed by Italy and India with 25&#160;GW and 15&#160;GW. The United Kingdom, France and Australia, would have installed capacities of close to 10&#160;GW each.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17</sup> IEA released this outlook in September 2014 <i>(see section below for more detail on the report)</i>. Two months later, however, India announced its intention to install 100&#160;GW of solar PV by 2022, and another six months later, SEIA forecasted that the United States would reach 40&#160;GW of cumulative PV capacity already by the end of 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-seia-05-2015_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seia-05-2015-57">[55]</a></sup> Furthermore, in July 2015, the UK was forecast to reach 10&#160;GW by early 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> IEA will release its next roadmap report on solar PV in 2018.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_long-term_forecast_.282050.29">Global long-term forecast (2050)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Global long-term forecast (2050)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014, the International Energy Agency (<a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">IEA</a>) released its latest edition of the <i>Technology Roadmap: Solar Photovoltaic Energy</i> report,<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-16">[16]</a></sup> calling for clear, credible and consistent signals from policy makers.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> The IEA also acknowledged to have previously underestimated PV deployment and reassessed its short-term and long-term goals.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><i>IEA report Technology Roadmap: Solar Photovoltaic Energy (September 2014)</i><sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup> —</p>
<dl>
<dd>Much has happened since our 2010 IEA technology roadmap for PV energy. PV has been deployed faster than anticipated and by 2020 will probably reach twice the level previously expected. Rapid deployment and falling costs have each been driving the other. This progress, together with other important changes in the energy landscape, notably concerning the status and progress of nuclear power and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">CCS</a>, have led the IEA to reassess the role of solar PV in mitigating climate change. This updated roadmap envisions PV's share of global electricity rising up to 16% by 2050, compared with 11% in the 2010 roadmap.</dd>
</dl>
</blockquote>
<p>IEA's long-term scenario for 2050 describes worldwide solar photovoltaics (PV) and concentrated solar thermal (<a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">CSP</a>) capacity to reach 4,600&#160;GW and 1,000&#160;GW, respectively. In order to achieve IEA's projection, PV deployment of 124&#160;GW and investments of $225 billion are required annually. This is about three and two times of current levels, respectively. By 2050, <a href="/wiki/Levelized_cost_of_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Levelized cost of electricity">levelized cost of electricity</a> (LCOE) generated by solar PV would cost between US 4¢ and 16¢ per kilowatt-hour (kWh), or by segment and on average, 5.6¢ per kWh for utility-scale power plants (range of 4¢ to 9.7¢), and 7.8¢ per kWh for solar rooftop systems (range of 4.9¢ to 15.9¢)<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5,24</sup> These estimates are based on a <a href="/wiki/Weighted_average_cost_of_capital" title="Weighted average cost of capital">weighted average cost of capital</a> (WACC) of 8%. The report notes that when the WACC exceeds 9%, over half the LCOE of PV is made of financial expenditures, and that more optimistic assumptions of a lower WACC would therefore significantly reduce the LCOE of solar PV in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:24–25</sup> The IEA also emphasizes that these new figures are not projections but rather scenarios they believe would occur if underlying economic, regulatory and political conditions played out.</p>
<p>In 2015, Fraunhofer ISE did a study commissioned by German renewable think tank <i>Agora Energiewende</i> and concluded that most scenarios fundamentally underestimate the role of solar power in future energy systems.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> Fraunhofer's study <i>(see summary of its conclusions below)</i> differs significantly form IEA's roadmap report on solar PV technology despite being published only a few months apart. The report foresees worldwide installed PV capacity to reach as much as 30,700&#160;GW by 2050. By then, Fraunhofer expects LCOE for utility-scale solar farms to reach €0.02 to €0.04 per kilowatt-hour, or about half of what the International Energy Agency has been projecting (4¢ to 9.7¢). Turnkey system costs would decrease by more than 50% to €436/kW<sub>p</sub> from currently €995/kW<sub>p</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-agora-feb-2015_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agora-feb-2015-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:67</sup> This is also noteworthy, as IEA's roadmap published significantly higher estimates of $1,400 to $3,300 per kW<sub>p</sub> for eight major markets around the world <i>(see table <a href="#Typical_PV_system_prices">Typical PV system prices in 2013</a> below)</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:15</sup> However, the study agrees with IEA's roadmap report by emphasizing the importance of the cost of capital (WACC), which strongly depends on regulatory regimes and may even outweigh local advantages of higher <a href="/wiki/Solar_insolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar insolation">solar insolation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-agora-feb-2015_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agora-feb-2015-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1, 53</sup> In the study, a WACC of 5%, 7.5% and 10% is used to calculate the projected levelized cost of electricity for utility-scale solar PV in 18 different markets worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-agora-feb-2015_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agora-feb-2015-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:65</sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><i>Fraunhofer ISE: Current and Future Cost of Photovoltaics. Long-term Scenarios for Market Development, System Prices and LCOE of Utility-Scale PV Systems. Study on behalf of Agora Energiewende (February 2015)</i><sup id="cite_ref-agora-feb-2015_48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agora-feb-2015-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup> —</p>
<ol>
<li>Solar photovoltaics is already today a low-cost renewable energy technology. Cost of power from large scale photovoltaic installations in Germany fell from over 40 ct/kWh in 2005 to 9 cts/kWh in 2014. Even lower prices have been reported in sunnier regions of the world, since a major share of cost components is traded on global markets.</li>
<li>Solar power will soon be the cheapest form of electricity in many regions of the world. Even in conservative scenarios and assuming no major technological breakthroughs, an end to cost reduction is not in sight. Depending on annual sunshine, power cost of 4–6 cts/kWh are expected by 2025, reaching 2–4 ct/kWh by 2050 (conservative estimate).</li>
<li>Financial and regulatory environments will be key to reducing cost in the future. Cost of hardware sourced from global markets will decrease irrespective of local conditions. However, inadequate regulatory regimes may increase cost of power by up to 50 percent through higher cost of finance. This may even overcompensate the effect of better local solar resources.</li>
<li>Most scenarios fundamentally underestimate the role of solar power in future energy systems. Based on outdated cost estimates, most scenarios modeling future domestic, regional or global power systems foresee only a small contribution of solar power. The results of our analysis indicate that a fundamental review of cost-optimal power system pathways is necessary.</li>
</ol>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_forecasts">Regional forecasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Regional forecasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:China_Photovoltaics_Installed_Capacity.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/China_Photovoltaics_Installed_Capacity.svg/220px-China_Photovoltaics_Installed_Capacity.svg.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/China_Photovoltaics_Installed_Capacity.svg/330px-China_Photovoltaics_Installed_Capacity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/China_Photovoltaics_Installed_Capacity.svg/440px-China_Photovoltaics_Installed_Capacity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:China_Photovoltaics_Installed_Capacity.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
PV capacity growth in <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>In October 2015, China's <a href="/wiki/National_Energy_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="National Energy Administration">National Energy Administration</a> (NDRC) set an ambitious 23.1&#160;GW target for 2015, upgrading its previous target of 17.8&#160;GW from March 2015, which was already more than the entire global PV capacity installed in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-CleanTechnica_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CleanTechnica-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> With this revised target, China will have surpassed Germany's total capacity of 40&#160;GW by the end of the year and become the world's largest overall producer of photovoltaic power.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>As of October 2015, China plans to install 150&#160;GW of solar power by 2020,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> an increase of 50&#160;GW compared to the 2020-target announced in October 2014, when China planned to install 100&#160;GW of solar power—along with 200&#160;GW of <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a>, 350&#160;GW of <a href="/wiki/Hydro_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydro power">hydro</a> and 58&#160;GW of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear</a> power.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>Overall, China has consistently increased its annual and short term targets. However estimates, targets and actual deployment have differed substantially in the past: in 2013 and 2014, China was expected to continue to install 10&#160;GW per year.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:37</sup> In February 2014, China's NDRC upgraded its 2014 target from 10&#160;GW to 14&#160;GW<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> (later adjusted to 13&#160;GW<sup id="cite_ref-IHS-2014-estimate_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-2014-estimate-65">[63]</a></sup>) and ended up installing an estimated 10.6&#160;GW due to shortcomings in the <a href="/wiki/Distributed_generation" title="Distributed generation">distributed</a> PV sector.<sup id="cite_ref-cleantechnica-china-2014_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cleantechnica-china-2014-66">[64]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_India" title="Solar power in India">India</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>By 2016, India plans to have constructed the <a href="/wiki/List_of_photovoltaic_power_stations#World.27s_largest_photovoltaic_power_stations" title="List of photovoltaic power stations">world’s largest solar farm</a> with a capacity of 750&#160;MW. The country plans to install 100&#160;GW capacity of solar power by 2022, a five-time increase from a previous target.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> However, India's solar PV deployment was below expectation and actually declined from 1,115&#160;MW in 2013 to 616&#160;MW in 2014, which contrasts with the country’s positive policy tone towards solar PV. In 2015, record installations are 3018 MW against expectation of 2&#160;GW.<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-may-2015_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-may-2015-43">[41]</a></sup> As of July 13, 2015, total commissioned capacity in India surpassed 4&#160;GW and stands at 4,096&#160;MW, with the state <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> taking the lead with 1,164&#160;MW and 1,000&#160;MW, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Japan" title="Solar power in Japan">Japan</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>For 2014, installations in Japan reached an all time record level of 9.7 GW, compared to 6.9&#160;GW in 2013. By the end of 2014, Japan's installed PV capacity of 23.3&#160;GW can now contribute about 2.5% to the overall domestic electricity demand.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:13</sup> In 2014, Japan also overtook Italy (18.5&#160;GW) as the world's third largest PV nation in terms of cumulative capacity. IHS forecasts that Japan will retain its position as the world's second largest solar market for new installations and grow by 4%, adding another 10.4&#160;GW in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-ihs-2015-03-30_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs-2015-03-30-40">[38]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">United States</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>In March 2015, SEIA, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_Energy_Industries_Association" title="Solar Energy Industries Association">Solar Energy Industries Association</a> and GTM Research, released their U.S. estimate for 2014. In the United States, a total of 6.2 gigawatts had been installed, up 30 percent over 2013 (vs. previous projection of 6.5&#160;GW in September 2014). This brings the country’s cumulative PV total to 18.3&#160;GW. However, according to IHS, U.S. deployment amounted to 7&#160;GW in 2014, or 800&#160;MW higher than SEIA reported.<sup id="cite_ref-ihs-2015-03-30_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs-2015-03-30-40">[38]</a></sup> In June 2014 <a href="/wiki/Barclays" title="Barclays">Barclays</a> downgraded bonds of U.S. utility companies. Barclays expects more competition by a growing self-consumption due to a combination of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_generation" title="Distributed generation">decentralized</a> PV-systems and residential <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">electricity storage</a>. This could fundamentally change the utility's business model and transform the system over the next ten years, as prices for these systems are predicted to fall.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> For 2015, annual PV installations are predicted to increase to 8.1&#160;GW (cumulative of 26.4&#160;GW) by the end of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-US-Solar-Market-Insight-2014_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US-Solar-Market-Insight-2014-41">[39]</a></sup> Other sources see U.S. deployment to increase by approximately 9&#160;GW in 2015, before peaking in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-2015-06-17_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-2015-06-17-34">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ihs-2015-03-30_40-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs-2015-03-30-40">[38]</a></sup> In May 2015, SEIA predicted U.S. PV-market to grow by 25% to 50% in 2016, with a cumulative PV capactiy of 40&#160;GW by the end of 2016. Roughly, this translates into 13&#160;GW of added capacity in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-seia-05-2015_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seia-05-2015-57">[55]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar power in the European Union">Europe</a></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2014.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2014.svg/180px-MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2014.svg.png" width="180" height="189" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2014.svg/270px-MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2014.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2014.svg/360px-MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2014.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="420" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2015.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2015.svg/180px-MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2015.svg.png" width="180" height="189" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2015.svg/270px-MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2015.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2015.svg/360px-MapEuropeSmall_WattPerCapita_2015.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="420" /></a></div>
</div>

</td>
</tr>
<tr style="vertical-align:top">
<td>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #8C4F26; background-color: #C87137; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;100–150</div>
</td>

</td>
<td>

</td>
<td>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #140C05; background-color: #502D16; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;300–450</div>
</td>
<td>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #140C05; background-color: #28170B; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;&gt;450</div>
</td>

</table>
<i>(also see <a href="/wiki/File:Europe_WattPerCapita_animated_sequence_2008-2013.gif" title="File:Europe WattPerCapita animated sequence 2008-2013.gif">animated map</a>, 1992–2014)</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>By 2020, the <a href="/wiki/European_Photovoltaic_Industry_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="European Photovoltaic Industry Association">European Photovoltaic Industry Association</a> (EPIA) expects PV capacity to pass 150&#160;GW. It finds the <a href="/wiki/European_Comission" class="mw-redirect" title="European Comission">EC</a>-supervised national action plans for renewables (<a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Action_Plan" title="National Renewable Energy Action Plan">NREAP</a>) turned out to be too conservative, as the goal of 84&#160;GW of solar PV by 2020 had already been surpassed in 2014 (prelim. figures account for close to 88&#160;GW by the end of 2014).<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup> For 2030, EPIA originally predicted solar PV to reach between 330 and 500&#160;GW, supplying 10 to 15 percent of Europe's electricity demand. However, recent reassessments are more pessimistic and point to a 7 to 11 percent share, if no major policy changes are undertaken.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:35</sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>In 2014, overall European markets continued to decline despite strong growth in some countries. These countries, with their percentage-increase of total capacity, were, the United Kingdom (+80%), the Netherlands (+54%), Switzerland (+42%), Austria (+22%) and France (+20%), which rebounced significantly in terms of annual installations from 643&#160;MW in 2013 to 927&#160;MW in 2014. In most countries, however, deployment underperformed or, in some cases, growth of cumulative capacity even fell below the one percent mark. In descending percentage-order, cumulative installations only grew by 6.0% in Romania (69&#160;MW), 5.2% in Germany (1,900&#160;MW), 2.1% in Italy (385&#160;MW), 2.1% in Belgium (65&#160;MW), 0.4% in Spain (22&#160;MW), 0.2% in Bulgaria (1.6&#160;MW), 0.08% in Slovakia (0.4&#160;MW), 0.06% in Greece (16&#160;MW), and 0.01% in the Czech Republic (1.7&#160;MW).<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:15</sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>The United Kingdom had the strongest percentage growth and became the fourth largest PV installer worldwide after China, Japan and the United States. In 2014, the country installed more than 2.2&#160;GW (vs. 1.1&#160;GW in 2013) and cumulative installation increased by 80% to 5.1&#160;GW by the end of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-DECC-statistics-PV_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DECC-statistics-PV-70">[68]</a></sup> The booming <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_stations" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic power stations">utility-scale installations</a> were partially explained by the upcoming closure of the appealing <a href="/wiki/Renewables_Obligation#Renewables_Obligation_Certificates" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewables Obligation">renewable obligation certificates</a> (ROC) scheme in March 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-IHS-2014-estimate_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-2014-estimate-65">[63]</a></sup> This may also bring another record deployment in 2015, as IHS forecasts 3.5 GW of installations for the year.<sup id="cite_ref-ihs-2015-03-30_40-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs-2015-03-30-40">[38]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>In Germany and Italy, the rate of new installations continued to decline in 2014 and is expected to remain unchanged or even decline to 1.3&#160;GW for 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-mercom-2015-06-17_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercom-2015-06-17-34">[32]</a></sup> In 2014, Germany installed 1,926 MW, down 36 percent from 3,300&#160;MW deployed in 2013. During the period of 2010–2012, the country was the world's leader installing more than 7&#160;GW annually. New cumulative capacity of 38.2&#160;GW corresponds to 475 watts per inhabitant. Italy installed 385&#160;MW,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> much less than previously expected and down from 1.5&#160;GW deployed in 2013. Overall capacity of 18.5&#160;GW translates into 304 watts per inhabitant. Solar PV now contributes significantly to domestic net-electricity consumption in Italy (7.9%), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Greece" title="Solar power in Greece">Greece</a> (7.6%) and Germany (7.0%).<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:15</sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_leading_countries">History of leading countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History of leading countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_solar_cells" title="Timeline of solar cells">Timeline of solar cells</a></div>
<p>Since the 1950s, when the first solar cells were commercially manufactured, there has been a succession of countries leading the world as the largest producer of electricity from solar photovoltaics. First it was the United States, then Japan, followed by Germany, and currently China.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_.281954.E2.80.931996.29">United States (1954–1996)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: United States (1954–1996)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">United States</a>, inventor of modern solar PV, was the leader of installed capacity for many years. Based on preceding work by Swedish and German engineers, the American engineer <a href="/wiki/Russell_Ohl" title="Russell Ohl">Russell Ohl</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a> patented the first modern solar cell in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> It was also there at Bell Labs where the first practical <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">c-silicon</a> cell was developed in 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hoffman_Electronics" class="mw-redirect" title="Hoffman Electronics">Hoffman Electronics</a>, the leading manufacturer of silicon solar cells in the 1950s and 1960s, improved on the cell's efficiency, produced solar radios, and equipped <a href="/wiki/Vanguard_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Vanguard I">Vanguard&#160;I</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Solar_panels_on_spacecraft" title="Solar panels on spacecraft">solar powered satellite</a> launched into orbit in 1958.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="width:370px;">
<div style="margin: 0px 15px -40px -50px; font-size: 85%;">
<div style="color: #000; font-size: 120%; padding: 10px 0 0 90px;"><b>PV capacity of leading countries</b> (MW)<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[c]</a></sup></div>
<div style="margin-top:1em;max-width:400px;" class="chart">
<div style="position:relative;min-height:260px;min-width:400px;max-width:400px;">

<div style="position:absolute;left:100px;top:176px;height:4px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:#CC00FF;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;overflow:hidden;" title="965 MW"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:150px;top:171px;height:9px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:#CC00FF;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;overflow:hidden;" title="1,901 MW"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:200px;top:165px;height:15px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:#CC00FF;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;overflow:hidden;" title="3,377 MW"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:250px;top:157px;height:23px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:#CC00FF;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;overflow:hidden;" title="5,104 MW"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:7px;top:174px;height:6px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:#1A1AFF;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;overflow:hidden;" title="1,255.7 MW"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:57px;top:169px;height:11px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:#1A1AFF;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;overflow:hidden;" title="2,528 MW"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:107px;top:160px;height:20px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:#1A1AFF;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;overflow:hidden;" title="4,383 MW"></div>

<div style="text-align: center;line-height: 1.8em;">Year by year cumulative capacities of important markets<br />
<span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:#CC00FF; color:#CC00FF;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;UK</span>&#160;&#160;&#160;<span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:#1A1AFF; color:#1A1AFF;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;USA</span>&#160;&#160;&#160;<span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:#FFCC00; color:#FFCC00;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Japan</span>&#160;&#160;&#160;<span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:red; color:red;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;China</span>&#160;&#160;&#160;<span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:#33CC4D; color:#33CC4D;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Italy</span>&#160;&#160;&#160;<span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:#4D4D4D; color:#4D4D4D;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Germany</span></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1977 US-President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> installed <a href="/wiki/Solar_hot_water_panel" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar hot water panel">solar hot water panels</a> on the White House promoting <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a><sup id="cite_ref-whitehouse-panels_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitehouse-panels-77">[74]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a>, originally named <i>Solar Energy Research Institute</i> was established at Golden, Colorado. In the 1980s and early 1990s, most photovoltaic modules were used in <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">stand-alone power systems</a> or powered consumer products such as <a href="/wiki/Solar-powered_watch" title="Solar-powered watch">watches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar-powered_calculator" title="Solar-powered calculator">calculators</a> and toys, but from around 1995, industry efforts have focused increasingly on developing grid-connected <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_PV_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Rooftop PV system">rooftop PV systems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">power stations</a>. By 1996, solar PV capacity in the US amounted to 77&#160;megawatts–more than any other country in the world at the time. Then, Japan stayed ahead.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan_.281997.E2.80.932004.29">Japan (1997–2004)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Japan (1997–2004)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Japan" title="Solar power in Japan">Japan</a> took the lead as the world's largest producer of PV electricity, after the city of Kobe was hit by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Hanshin_earthquake" title="Great Hanshin earthquake">Great Hanshin earthquake</a> in 1995. Kobe experienced severe power outages in the aftermath of the earthquake, and <a href="/wiki/PV_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="PV systems">PV systems</a> were then considered as a temporary supplier of power during such events, as the disruption of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_grid" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric grid">electric grid</a> paralyzed the entire infrastructure, including gas stations that depended on electricity to pump gasoline. Moreover, in December of that same year, an accident occurred at the multibillion-dollar experimental <a href="/wiki/Monju_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Monju Nuclear Power Plant">Monju Nuclear Power Plant</a>. A sodium leak caused a major fire and forced a shutdown (classified as <a href="/wiki/International_Nuclear_Event_Scale" title="International Nuclear Event Scale">INES</a> 1). There was massive public outrage when it was revealed that the semigovernmental agency in charge of Monju had tried to cover up the extent of the accident and resulting damage.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> Japan remained world leader in photovoltaics until 2004, when its capacity amounted to 1,132&#160;megawatts. Then, focus on PV deployment shifted to Europe.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany_.282005.E2.80.932014.29">Germany (2005–2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Germany (2005–2014)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Germany" title="Solar power in Germany">Germany</a> took the lead from Japan. With the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/German_Renewable_Energy_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="German Renewable Energy Act">Renewable Energy Act</a> in 2000, <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariffs" class="mw-redirect" title="Feed-in tariffs">feed-in tariffs</a> were adopted as a policy mechanism. This policy established that renewables have priority on the grid, and that a fixed price must be paid for the produced electricity over a 20-year period, providing a guaranteed return on investment irrespective of actual market prices. As a consequence, a high level of investment security lead to a soaring number of new photovoltaic installations that peaked in 2011, while investment costs in renewable technologies were brought down considerably. Germany's installed PV capacity is now approaching the 40,000&#160;megawatt mark.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China_.282015.E2.80.93present.29">China (2015–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: China (2015–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>China surpassed Germany's capacity by the end of 2015, becoming the world's largest producer of photovoltaic power.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a>'s rapid PV growth continued in 2016 - with 34.2GW of solar photovoltaics installed.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> The <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.reuters.com/article/us-china-renewables-tariffs-idUSKBN0U703Y20151224">quickly lowering feed in tariff rates</a> at the end of 2015 motivated many developers to secure tariff rates before mid-year 2016 - as they were anticipating further cuts (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.renewableenergyworld.com/articles/2016/10/china-to-lower-feed-in-tariff-cut-subsidies-for-solar-pv-systems.html">correctly so</a>). During the course of the year, China announced its goal of installing 100GW during the next <a href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_China" title="Five-year plans of China">Chinese Five Year Economic Plan</a> (2016-2020). China expects to spend ¥1 trillion ($145B) on solar construction<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> during that period.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_market_development">History of market development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: History of market development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prices_and_costs_.281977.E2.80.93present.29">Prices and costs (1977–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Prices and costs (1977–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">

<p>The average <a href="/wiki/Price_per_watt" title="Price per watt">price per watt</a> has dropped drastically for solar cells over the last few decades. While in 1977 prices for <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> cells were about $77 per watt, average spot prices in June 2014 were as low as $0.36 per watt or 200 times less than almost forty years ago. Prices for <a href="/wiki/Thin-film_solar_cell" title="Thin-film solar cell">thin-film solar cells</a> and for c-Si <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> were around $.60 per watt.<sup id="cite_ref-energytrend-pv-spotprice_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energytrend-pv-spotprice-84">[81]</a></sup> In 2015, module and cell prices declined even further <i>(see price quotes in table)</i>.</p>
<p>This price trend is seen as evidence supporting <a href="/wiki/Swanson%27s_law" title="Swanson's law">Swanson's law</a>, an observation similar to the famous <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Moore's Law">Moore's Law</a> that states that the per-watt cost of solar cells and panels fall by 20 percent for every doubling of cumulative photovoltaic production.<sup id="cite_ref-Econ1_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Econ1-85">[82]</a></sup> A 2015 study shows price/kWh dropping by 10% per year since 1980, and predicts that solar could contribute 20% of total electricity consumption by 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Typical_PV_system_prices"></span> In its 2014 edition of the <i>Technology Roadmap: Solar Photovoltaic Energy</i> report, the International Energy Agency (IEA) published prices for residential, commercial and utility-scale <a href="/wiki/PV_system" class="mw-redirect" title="PV system">PV systems</a> for eight major markets as of 2013 <i>(see table below)</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-16">[16]</a></sup> However, IEA's figures for the U.S seem to be controversial, as DOE's <a href="/wiki/SVTC_Technologies#The_SunShot_Initiative" title="SVTC Technologies">SunShot Initiative</a> report states lower prices, although being published at the same time and referring to the same period. Prices have since fallen further. For 2014, the SunShot Initiative modeled U.S. system prices to be in the range of $1.80 to $3.29 per watt.<sup id="cite_ref-sunshot-2014_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sunshot-2014-87">[84]</a></sup> Other sources identify similar price ranges of $1.70 to $3.50 for the different market segments in the U.S.,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> and in the highly penetrated German market, prices for residential and small commercial rooftop systems of up to 100&#160;kW declined to $1.36 per watt (€1.24/W) by the end of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> In 2015, Deutsche Bank estimated costs for small residential rooftop systems in the U.S. around $2.90 per watt. Costs for utility-scale systems in China and India were estimated as low as $1.00 per watt.<sup id="cite_ref-deutsche-2015-chasm_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deutsche-2015-chasm-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9</sup> As of mid-2015, a residential 5&#160;kW-system in Australia costs on average about AU$1.60, or US$1.23 per watt.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup></p>
