<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technologies_.281990.E2.80.93present.29">Technologies (1990–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Technologies (1990–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_Market_Share_by_PV_Technology_from_1990_to_2013.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Global_Market_Share_by_PV_Technology_from_1990_to_2013.svg/280px-Global_Market_Share_by_PV_Technology_from_1990_to_2013.svg.png" width="280" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Global_Market_Share_by_PV_Technology_from_1990_to_2013.svg/420px-Global_Market_Share_by_PV_Technology_from_1990_to_2013.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Global_Market_Share_by_PV_Technology_from_1990_to_2013.svg/560px-Global_Market_Share_by_PV_Technology_from_1990_to_2013.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1252" data-file-height="810" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_Market_Share_by_PV_Technology_from_1990_to_2013.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Market-share of PV technologies since 1990</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the advances in conventional <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> (c-Si) technology in recent years, and the falling cost of the <a href="/wiki/Polysilicon" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysilicon">polysilicon</a> since 2009, that followed after a period of severe shortage <i>(see below)</i> of silicon feedstock, pressure increased on manufacturers of commercial <a href="/wiki/Thin_film_solar_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Thin film solar cell">thin-film PV technologies</a>, including amorphous thin-film silicon (a-Si), cadmium telluride (CdTe), and copper indium gallium diselenide (CIGS), leading to the bankruptcy of several, once highly touted thin-film companies.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup> The sector continues to face price competition from Chinese crystalline silicon cell and module manufacturers, and some companies together with their patents were sold below cost.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT01913_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT01913-92">[89]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">
<div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden">
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:#4fc1cf"></div>
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:0; border-width:0 314.47802847242px 100px 0; border-right-color:#3300a6"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:#3300a6"></div>
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:45.887874787312px; border-width:0 0 54.112125212688px 84.094458229817px; border-bottom-color:yellow"></div>
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:56.854395431904px; border-width:0 0 43.145604568096px 90.21339593682px; border-bottom-color:#00CC00"></div>
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:68.501348034469px; border-width:0 0 31.498651965531px 94.909614499029px; border-bottom-color:#de2821"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:0;top:0"><img alt="Circle frame.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/200px-Circle_frame.svg.png" width="200" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/300px-Circle_frame.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/400px-Circle_frame.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="200" /></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>Global PV market by technology in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Pie-Chart-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pie-Chart-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18,19</sup></p>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #de2821; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Cadmium_telluride_photovoltaics" title="Cadmium telluride photovoltaics">CdTe</a> (5.1%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00CC00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon" title="Amorphous silicon">a-Si</a> (2.0%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:yellow; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Copper_indium_gallium_selenide_solar_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper indium gallium selenide solar cell">CIGS</a> (2.0%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #3300a6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Monocrystalline_silicon" title="Monocrystalline silicon">mono-Si</a> (36.0%)</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #4fc1cf; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Polycrystalline_silicon" title="Polycrystalline silicon">multi-Si</a> (54.9%)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2013 thin-film technologies accounted for about 9 percent of worldwide deployment, while 91 percent was held by crystalline silicon (<a href="/wiki/Mono-Si" class="mw-redirect" title="Mono-Si">mono-Si</a> and <a href="/wiki/Multi-Si" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-Si">multi-Si</a>). With 5 percent of the overall market, CdTe holds more than half of the thin-film market, leaving 2 percent to each, CIGS and amorphous silicon.<sup id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:24–25</sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Copper_indium_gallium_selenide_solar_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper indium gallium selenide solar cell">CIGS technology</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>Copper indium gallium selenide (CIGS) is the name of the semiconductor material the technology is based on. One of the largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_CIGS_companies" title="List of CIGS companies">producers of CIGS photovoltaics</a> is the Japanese company <a href="/wiki/Solar_Frontier" title="Solar Frontier">Solar Frontier</a> with a manufacturing capacity in the gigawatt-scale. The latest CIS line technology includes modules with <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell_efficiency" title="Solar cell efficiency">conversion efficiencies</a> of over 15%.<sup id="cite_ref-solar-frontier-apr-2015_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solar-frontier-apr-2015-95">[92]</a></sup> The company profits from the booming Japanese market and attempts to widen its international business. However, several prominent manufacturers couldn't stand the pressure caused by advances in conventional crystalline silicon technology of recent years. The company <a href="/wiki/Solyndra" title="Solyndra">Solyndra</a> ceased all business activity and filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy in 2011, and <a href="/wiki/Nanosolar" title="Nanosolar">Nanosolar</a>, also a CIGS manufacturer, closed its doors in 2013. Although both companies produced CIGS solar cells, it has been pointed out, that the failure was not due to the technology but rather because of the companies themselves, using a flawed architecture, such as, for example, Solyndra's cylindrical substrates.<sup id="cite_ref-spw-cigs-simplified_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spw-cigs-simplified-96">[93]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cadmium_telluride_photovoltaics" title="Cadmium telluride photovoltaics">CdTe technology</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The U.S.-company <a href="/wiki/First_Solar" title="First Solar">First Solar</a>, a leading manufacturer of CdTe, has been building several of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_photovoltaic_power_stations" title="List of photovoltaic power stations">world's largest solar power stations</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Desert_Sunlight_Solar_Farm" title="Desert Sunlight Solar Farm">Desert Sunlight Solar Farm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz Solar Farm</a>, both in the Californian desert with a staggering 550&#160;MW capacity each, as well as the 102 MW<sub><a href="/wiki/Watt-peak_AC" class="mw-redirect" title="Watt-peak AC">AC</a></sub> Nyngan Solar Plant in <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Australia" title="Solar power in Australia">Australia</a>, the largest PV power station in the Southern Hemnisphere, commissioned in mid-2015.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup> The company successfully produces CdTe-panels with a steadily increasing efficiency and declining cost per watt, as reported in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18–19</sup> CdTe has the lowest <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon#Energy_payback_time" title="Crystalline silicon">energy payback time</a> of all mass-produced PV technologies, and can be as short as eight months in favorable locations.<sup id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:31</sup> The company <a href="/wiki/Abound_Solar" title="Abound Solar">Abound Solar</a>, also a manufacturer of cadmium telluride modules, went bankrupt in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thin_film_solar_cell#Amorphous_silicon" class="mw-redirect" title="Thin film solar cell">a-Si technology</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>In 2012, <a href="/wiki/Energy_Conversion_Devices" title="Energy Conversion Devices">ECD solar</a>, once one of the world's leading manufacturer of amorphous silicon (a-Si) technology, filed for bankruptcy in Michigan, United States. Swiss <a href="/wiki/OC_Oerlikon" title="OC Oerlikon">OC Oerlikon</a> divested its <a href="/wiki/Oerlikon_Solar" class="mw-redirect" title="Oerlikon Solar">solar division</a> that produced a-Si/μc-Si tandem cells to <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Electron_Limited" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokyo Electron Limited">Tokyo Electron Limited</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> In 2014, the Japanese electronics and semiconductor company announced the closure of its <a href="/wiki/Micromorph" title="Micromorph">micromorph</a> technology development program.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> Other companies that left the amorphous silicon thin-film market include <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a>, <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a>, Flexcell, Inventux, Pramac, Schuco, Sencera, EPV Solar,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> NovaSolar (formerly OptiSolar)<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Suntech_Power" title="Suntech Power">Suntech Power</a> that stopped manufacturing a-Si modules in 2010 to focus on <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> solar panels. In 2013, Suntech filed for bankruptcy in China.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-106">[103]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicon_shortage_.282005.E2.80.932008.29">Silicon shortage (2005–2008)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Silicon shortage (2005–2008)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polysilicon_prices_history_since_2004.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Polysilicon_prices_history_since_2004.svg/220px-Polysilicon_prices_history_since_2004.svg.png" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Polysilicon_prices_history_since_2004.svg/330px-Polysilicon_prices_history_since_2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Polysilicon_prices_history_since_2004.svg/440px-Polysilicon_prices_history_since_2004.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="745" data-file-height="919" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polysilicon_prices_history_since_2004.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Polysilicon" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysilicon">Polysilicon</a> prices since 2004. As of October 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Average_selling_price" title="Average selling price">ASP</a> for polysilicon stands at $15.30/kg<sup id="cite_ref-energytrend-pricequotes_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energytrend-pricequotes-83">[80]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early 2000s, prices for <a href="/wiki/Polysilicon" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysilicon">polysilicon</a>, the raw material for conventional solar cells, were as low as $30 per kilogram and silicon manufacturers had initially no incentive to expand production by additional investments.</p>
<p>However, a severe silicon shortage came along in 2005, when governmental programmes sparked the deployment of solar PV to rise by 75% in Europe. In addition, the demand for silicon from semiconductor manufacturers was growing as well. Since the amount of silicon needed for semiconductors makes up a much smaller portion of production costs, manufacturers were able to outbid solar companies for the available silicon in the market.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially, the incumbent polysilicon producers were slow to respond to rising demand for solar applications, because of their painful experiences with over-investment in the past. Silicon prices sharply rose to about $80 per kilogram, and reached as much as $400/kg for long-term contracts and spot prices. In 2007, the constraints on silicon became so severe that the solar industry was forced to idle about a quarter of its cell and module manufacturing capacity—an estimated 777&#160;MW of the then available production capacity. The shortage also provided silicon specialists with both the cash and an incentive to develop new technologies and several new producers entered the market. Early responses from the solar industry focused on improvements in the recycling of silicon. When this potential was exhausted, companies have been taking a harder look at alternatives to the conventional <a href="/wiki/Siemens_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens process">Siemens process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>As it takes about three years to build a new polysilicon plant, the shortage prolonged until 2008. Prices for conventional solar cells remained constant or even rose slightly during the period of silicon shortage from 2005 to 2008. This is notably seen as a "shoulder" that sticks out in the <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Swansons-law.png" class="extiw" title="commons:File:Swansons-law.png">Swanson's PV-learning curve</a> and it was feared that a prolonged shortage could delay solar power to become competitive with conventional energy prices without subsidies.</p>
<p>In the meantime the solar industry lowered the number of grams-per-watt by reducing wafer thickness and kerf loss, increased yields in all manufacturing steps, reducing module loss, and continuously raised panel efficiency. Finally, the ramp up of polysilicon production alleviated worldwide markets from the scarcity of silicon in 2009 and subsequently lead to an overcapacity with sharply declining prices in the photovoltaic industry for the following years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_overcapacity_.282009.E2.80.932013.29">Solar overcapacity (2009–2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Solar overcapacity (2009–2013)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:237px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="width:235px;">
<div style="padding-top: 10px; text-align: center;"><b>Solar module production</b></div>
<div style="padding-top:0px; text-align: center; font-size: 0.85em;"><b>utilization of production capacity in&#160;%</b></div>
<div style="font-size: 80%;">
<div class="timeline-wrapper"><map name="timeline_9a320bda443675a5a76c0fc59c048a0e" id="timeline_9a320bda443675a5a76c0fc59c048a0e"></map><img usemap="#timeline_9a320bda443675a5a76c0fc59c048a0e" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/9a320bda443675a5a76c0fc59c048a0e.png" /></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">Utilization rate of <a href="/wiki/Solar_PV_module" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar PV module">solar PV module</a> production capacity in&#160;% since 1993<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:47</sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/Polycrystalline_silicon#Manufacturers" title="Polycrystalline silicon">polysilicon</a> industry had started to build additional large production capacities during the shortage period, prices dropped as low as $15 per kilogram forcing some producers to suspend production or exit the sector. Since then, prices for silicon have stabilized around $20 per kilogram and the booming solar PV market has also helped to reduce the enormous global overcapacity since 2009. However, overcapacity in the PV industry continues to persist. In 2013, global record deployment of 38&#160;GW (updated EPIA figure<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup>) was still much lower than China's annual production capacity of approximately 60&#160;GW. Continued overcapacity was further reduced by significantly lowering <a href="/wiki/Solar_module" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar module">solar module</a> prices and, as a consequence, many manufacturers could no longer cover costs or remain competitive. As worldwide growth of PV deployment continues and will likely break another record in 2014, the gap between overcapacity and global demand is expected to close in the next few years.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>IEA-PVPS published historical data for the worldwide utilization of solar PV module production capacity that displays a slow return to normalization in manufacture in recent years. The utilization rate is the ratio of production capacities versus actual production output for a given year. A low of 49% was reached in 2007 and reflects the peak of the silicon shortage that idled a significant share of the module production capacity. As of 2013, the utilization rate had recovered somewhat and increased to 63%.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:47</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-dumping_duties_.282012.E2.80.93present.29">Anti-dumping duties (2012–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Anti-dumping duties (2012–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After anti-dumping petition were filed and investigations carried out,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup> the United States imposed tariffs of 31 percent to 250 percent on solar products imported from China in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup> A year later, the EU also imposed definitive anti-dumping and anti-subsidy measures on imports of solar panels from China at an average of 47.7 percent for a two-year time span.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly thereafter, China, in turn, levied duties on U.S. polysilicon imports, the feedstock for the production of solar cells.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup> In January 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Ministry_of_Commerce" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Ministry of Commerce">Chinese Ministry of Commerce</a> set its anti-dumping tariff on U.S. polysilicon producers, such as Hemlock Semiconductor Corporation to 57%, while other <a href="/wiki/Polycrystalline_silicon#Manufacturers" title="Polycrystalline silicon">major polysilicon producing companies</a>, such as German Wacker Chemie and Korean OCI were much less affected.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup> All this has caused much controversy between proponents and opponents and is subject of current debate.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_deployment">History of deployment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: History of deployment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Deployment figures on a global, regional and nationwide scale are well documented since the early 1990s. While worldwide photovoltaic capacity has been growing continuously, deployment figures by country are much more dynamic, as they depend strongly on national policies. A number of organizations release comprehensive reports on PV deployment on a yearly basis. They include annual and cumulative deployed PV <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">capacity</a>, typically given in <a href="/wiki/Watt-peak" class="mw-redirect" title="Watt-peak">watt-peak</a>, a break-down by markets, as well as in-depth analysis and forecasts about future trends.</p>

<div class="thumbcaption">Annual PV deployment as a&#160;%-share of global total capacity <i>(projection for 2015)</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2015_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup></div>

<p>Due to the exponential nature of PV deployment, about 83 percent of the overall capacity has been installed during the last five years from 2011 to 2015 <i>(see pie-chart; projected figure for 2015)</i>. Since the 1990s, and except for 2012, each year has been a record-breaking year in terms of newly installed PV capacity.</p>

<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide_cumulative">Worldwide cumulative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Worldwide cumulative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PV_cume_semi_log_chart_2014_estimate.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/PV_cume_semi_log_chart_2014_estimate.svg/220px-PV_cume_semi_log_chart_2014_estimate.svg.png" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/PV_cume_semi_log_chart_2014_estimate.svg/330px-PV_cume_semi_log_chart_2014_estimate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/PV_cume_semi_log_chart_2014_estimate.svg/440px-PV_cume_semi_log_chart_2014_estimate.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="627" data-file-height="365" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PV_cume_semi_log_chart_2014_estimate.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Worldwide cumulative PV capacity on a semi log chart since 1992</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Worldwide growth of solar PV capacity has been fitting an exponential curve since 1992. Tables below show global cumulative nominal capacity by the end of each year in <a href="/wiki/Megawatts" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatts">megawatts</a>, and the year-to-year increase in percent. In 2014, global capacity is expected to grow by 33 percent from 138,856 to 185,000 MW. This corresponds to an exponential growth rate of 29 percent or about 2.4 years for current worldwide PV capacity to <a href="/wiki/Doubling_time" title="Doubling time">double</a>. Exponential growth rate: P(t) = P<sub>0</sub>e<sup>rt</sup>, where <i>P<sub>0</sub></i> is 139&#160;GW, growth-rate <i>r</i> 0.29 (results in <a href="/wiki/Doubling_time" title="Doubling time">doubling time</a> <i>t</i> of 2.4 years).</p>
<p>The following table contains data from four different sources. For 1992–1995: compiled figures of 16 main markets <i>(see section <a href="#All_time_PV_installations_by_country">All time PV installations by country</a>)</i>. For 1996–1999: <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a>-Statistical Review of world energy (Historical Data Workbook)<sup id="cite_ref-BP-review-world-energy_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-review-world-energy-117">[113]</a></sup> for 2000–2013: EPIA Global Outlook on Photovoltaics Report<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17</sup> and for 2014, preliminary figures are based on IEA-PVPS' snapshot report<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup></p>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deployment_reports">Deployment reports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Deployment reports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most PV deployment figures in this article are provided by the European Photovoltaic Industry Association in the "Global Outlook for Photovoltaics" report, the <i>Observatoire des énergies renouvelables</i> or EurObserv'ER's "Photovoltaic Barometer" report, and the IEA-PVPS (photovoltaic power systems) "Snapshot" and "Trends" report.</p>

<li>2008 – Global Market Outlook for Photovoltaics until 2012<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2008_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2008-124">[120]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>The European Photovoltaic Industry Association (EPIA) represents members of the entire PV industry from silicon producers to cells and module manufactures and PV systems installers to PV electricity generation as well as marketing and sales. EPIA releases its annual <i>Global Market Outlook for Photovoltaics</i> report in May/June.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>PV-Barometer</li>
</ul>

</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>EUROBSER'VER (<i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Observatoire des énergies renouvelables</span></i>) was set up in 1980, and is composed of engineers and experts releasing the <i>Photovoltaic Barometer</i> report containing early, year-end PV deployment figures for the 28 member states of the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[127]</a></sup> Eurobserver works closely together with several French ministries and is co-founded by the European Commission's IEE programm.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[128]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/IEA-PVPS" class="mw-redirect" title="IEA-PVPS">IEA-PVPS</a></li>
</ul>
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;">
<div class="NavHead" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color: #f2f2f2; text-align: left; padding-left: 5px; width: 400px; font-style: italic; font-size: 0.85em; margin-left: 16px;">List of reports - PVPS Snapshot of Global PV <i>and</i> Trends&#160;</div>
<div class="NavContent" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-left: 10px;">
<ul>
<li>2015 – Snapshot of Global PV 1992-2014<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_4-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
<li>2014 – Trends 2014 in Photovoltaic Applications – Survey report of selected IEA countries between 1992 and 2013<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-109">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>2014 – Snapshot of Global PV 1992-2013<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2013_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2013-17">[17]</a></sup></li>

<li>2013 – Snapshot of Global PV 1992-2012<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2012_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2012-134">[130]</a></sup></li>
<li>2011 – Trends in Photovoltaic Applications – Survey report of selected IEA countries between 1992 and 2010<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2011_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2011-135">[131]</a></sup></li>
<li>2010 – Trends in Photovoltaic Applications – Survey report of selected IEA countries between 1992 and 2009<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2010_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2010-136">[132]</a></sup></li>
<li>2009 – Trends in Photovoltaic Applications – Survey report of selected IEA countries between 1992 and 2008<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2009_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2009-137">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li>2007 – Trends in Photovoltaic Applications – Survey report of selected IEA countries between 1992 and 2006<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2007_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2007-138">[134]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">IEA</a> Photovoltaic Power Systems Programme (PVPS) is one of the collaborative R&amp;D agreements established within the IEA and, since its establishment in 1993, the PVPS participants have been conducting a variety of joint projects in the application of photovoltaic conversion of solar energy into electricity. Its annual "Snapshot" report is released in early April and provides the first and detailed figures of worldwide PV-deployment of the previous year. An overview of all international statistics PDF reports since 1995 can be found on IEA-PVPS' <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.iea-pvps.org/index.php?id=92">Statistic Reports</a> website.</dd>
</dl>

<dl>
<dt>2013</dt>
</dl>
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;width: 94%; margin-left: 19px;">
<div class="NavHead" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;font-size: 1.15em; padding: 5px 5px 5px 115px; text-align: left; border: 1px #b2b2b2 solid; background-color: #f2f2f2;">2013 – Year-end PV capacity by country in MW&#160;</div>
<div class="NavContent" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;">
<dl>
<dt>Worldwide</dt>
</dl>
<p>In 2013, worldwide deployment of solar PV amounted to almost 40&#160;GW (39,953&#160;MW)—an increase of about 35 percent over the previous year. Cumulated capacity increased by 38 percent to more than 139&#160;GW. This is sufficient to generate at least 160 <a href="/wiki/Terawatt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Terawatt-hour">terawatt-hours</a> (TWh) or 0.85 percent of the world's total <a href="/wiki/Electricity_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="Electricity consumption">electricity consumption</a> of 18,400 TWh.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2013_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2013-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5,13</sup><sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9</sup></p>

<div style="font-size: 0.9em; margin: 0 12px 12px 6px;">An uncertainty in Chinese deployment was the reason for a significant discrepancy between EPIA's <i>outlook</i> and IEA's <i>Trends</i> reports. EPIA did not take 1.1&#160;GW of additional Chinese deployment into account, since at the time it was unsure whether these installations were connected to the grid.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup> In IEA's final <i>Trends</i> report, the confirmed figures not only raised domestic Chinese deployment from 11.8&#160;GW to 12.92&#160;GW, but also contributed to increase global figures to almost 40&#160;GW.</div>
<dl>
<dt>Regions</dt>
</dl>
<p>In 2013, Asia has been the fastest growing region, with China and Japan accounting for 49% of worldwide deployment. About a quarter has been installed in Europe (10,975&#160;MW). The remaining quarter of the 38,400&#160;MW deployed in 2013 is split between North America and other countries.</p>
<p>Europe is still the most developed region with a cumulative capacity of 81.5&#160;GW, about 59 percent of the global total, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific" title="Asia-Pacific">Asia-Pacific</a> region (APAC), including countries such as Japan, India and Australia with 22&#160;GW or about 16 percent of worldwide cumulative capacity (due to its significance, China is excluded from the APAC region in all PV statistics and listed separately). European solar PV now covers 3 percent of the electricity demand and 6 percent of the peak electricity demand. However, deployment in Europe has slowed down by half compared to the record year of 2011, and will most likely continue to decrease. This is mainly due to the strong decline of new installations in Germany and Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18</sup></p>
<p>Cumulative capacity in the MEA (Middle East and Africa) region and ROW (rest of the world) accounted for less than 3&#160;GW or about 2.2% of the global total. A great untapped potential remains for many of these countries, especially in the Sunbelt.<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:19,23</sup></p>

</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2013, the world's top installer were <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a> (+12.92&#160;GW), followed by <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Japan" title="Solar power in Japan">Japan</a> (+6.97&#160;GW) and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">United States</a> (+4.75&#160;GW), while Germany remained the world's largest overall producer of power from solar PV, with a total capacity around 36&#160;GW and contributing 5.7% to its net electricity consumption. Italy met more than 7% of its electricity demands with solar PV, thus leading the world in that respect.</p>

<p>The top ten leading countries in terms of deployed and overall PV-capacity are shown above. Other mentionable PV deployments above the 100-megawatt mark included <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_France" title="Solar power in France">France</a> (643&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Canada" title="Solar power in Canada">Canada</a> (445&#160;MW), South Korea (445&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Thailand" title="Solar power in Thailand">Thailand</a> (437&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_Netherlands" title="Solar power in the Netherlands">The Netherlands</a> (360&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Switzerland" title="Solar power in Switzerland">Switzerland</a> (319&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Ukraine" title="Solar power in Ukraine">Ukraine</a> (290&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Austria" title="Solar power in Austria">Austria</a> (263&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Israel" title="Solar power in Israel">Israel</a> (244&#160;MW), <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Belgium" title="Solar power in Belgium">Belgium</a> (237&#160;MW), Taiwan (170&#160;MW) <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Denmark" title="Solar power in Denmark">Denmark</a> (156&#160;MW) and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Spain" title="Solar power in Spain">Spain</a> (116&#160;MW).<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_109-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:33–34</sup></p>

<p><sup>(A)</sup>&#160;<b>Data sources</b> (<a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">Nameplate capacity</a> in <a href="/wiki/Watt-peak_AC" class="mw-redirect" title="Watt-peak AC">DC-output not AC</a>):</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">IEA</a>-<a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency#Promotion_of_renewable_energy" title="International Energy Agency">PVPS</a> — Trends 2014 in Photovoltaic Applications<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_109-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:33–34</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/European_Photovoltaic_Industry_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="European Photovoltaic Industry Association">EPIA</a> — Global Market Outlook for Photovoltaics 2014–2018<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:24</sup></li>
<li>Find additional or alternative information in column <i>source</i> of corresponding record</li>
</ul>
</th>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In 2013, Europe added 11 gigawatts of new PV installation (including non-EU countries). It is still the most developed region with a cumulated total of 81.5 GW, about 59 percent of the worldwide installed capacity. Solar PV now covers 3 percent of the electricity demand and 6 percent of the peak electricity demand. However, European PV deployment has slowed down by half compared to the record year of 2011, and will most likely continue to decrease. This is mainly due to the strong decline of new installations in Germany and Italy.</p>

</div>
<dl>
<dt>2007</dt>
</dl>
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;width: 94%; margin-left: 19px;">
<div class="NavHead" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;font-size: 1.15em; padding: 5px 5px 5px 115px; text-align: left; border: 1px #b2b2b2 solid; background-color: #f2f2f2;">2007 – Year-end PV capacity by country in MW&#160;</div>
<div class="NavContent" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;">
<p>Off grid refers to photovoltaics which are not grid connected. On grid means connected to the local electricity grid. Δ means the amount installed during the previous year. Σ means the total amount installed. Wp/capita refers to the ratio of total installed capacity divided by total population, or total installed Wp per person. Module price is average installed price, in Euros. kW·h/kWp·yr indicates the range of insolation to be expected. While National Report(s) may be cited as source(s) within an International Report, any contradictions in data are resolved by using only the most recent report's data. Exchange rates represent the 2006 annual average of daily rates (OECD Main Economic Indicators June 2007).<br />
Module Price: Lowest:2.5 EUR/Wp (2.83 USD/Wp<sup id="cite_ref-Exchange_Rates_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exchange_Rates-146">[138]</a></sup>) in Germany 2003. Uncited insolation data is from maps dating 1991–1995.<sup id="cite_ref-PV_Power_2007-June_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PV_Power_2007-June-147">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baro178-PDF_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baro178-PDF-148">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baro178-ASP_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baro178-ASP-149">[141]</a></sup></p>

<dt>2005</dt>
</dl>
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;width: 94%; margin-left: 19px;">
<div class="NavHead" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;font-size: 1.15em; padding: 5px 5px 5px 115px; text-align: left; border: 1px #b2b2b2 solid; background-color: #f2f2f2;">2005 – Year-end PV capacity by country in MW&#160;</div>
<div class="NavContent" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;">
<p>Original source gives these individual numbers and totals them to 37,500&#160;kW. The 2004 reported total was 30,700&#160;kW.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[160]</a></sup> With new installations of 6,800&#160;kW, this would give the reported 37,500&#160;kW.</p>

<tr>
<td colspan="25" style="padding: 5px; text-align: left; font-size: 0.95em;"><b>Notes</b>:
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_discrepancy-LUX"><b><a href="#ref_discrepancy-LUX">^A</a></b></span> Strong discrepancy for <b>Luxembourg</b>: EPIA-figures report unchanged capacity of 30 MW for Y2011-2013 (source listed in row "References"), while Photovoltaic Barometer<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[165]</a></sup> reports a capacity of 76.7 MW for Y2012 and 100 MW for Y2013. Table displays EPIA figures.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_discrepancy-NOR"><b><a href="#ref_discrepancy-NOR">^B</a></b></span> Strong discrepancy for <b>Norway</b>: Figures based on <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a>-Statistical Review of world energy<sup id="cite_ref-BP-review-world-energy_117-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-review-world-energy-117">[113]</a></sup> and IEA-PVPS trend report|<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_109-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-109">[106]</a></sup> as EIPA outlook report<sup id="cite_ref-epia-2014_3-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:24</sup> mentions virtually zero deployment (as 0.02 watt per capita results in 0.07 MW).</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_spcl-source-CHL"><b><a href="#ref_spcl-source-CHL">^C</a></b></span> Different data source for <b>Chile</b>, figures based on reports<sup id="cite_ref-CORFO_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CORFO-142">[136]</a></sup> published by the Chilean Ministry of Energy—Centro de Energías Renovables (CER) and <a href="/wiki/CORFO" title="CORFO">CORFO</a>. Montly reports revise figures retroactively. Distinction between solar PV and CSP is missing, however.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_to-be-checked"><b><a href="#ref_to-be-checked">^D</a></b></span> Figures for <b>Brazil</b> and <b>Peru</b> need to be checked, as sources are unclear. Peru's 22 MW reflects capacity of one solar farm opened in 2012<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[167]</a></sup> Historical data for these countries may be verifiable when new reports are released.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_discrepancy-UK"><b><a href="#ref_discrepancy-UK">^E</a></b></span> Displayed IEA-PVPS/EPIA figures for the <b>United Kingdom</b> differ significantly from those published by <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Energy_and_Climate_Change" title="Department of Energy and Climate Change">DECC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DECC-statistics-PV_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DECC-statistics-PV-70">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[168]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_latinamerica"><b><a href="#ref_latinamerica">^F</a></b></span> Only fragmented figures for all Central American and some Latin American countries available. Based on public figures from GTM's <i>Latin America PV Playbook</i><sup id="cite_ref-gtm-latinameric-2015_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gtm-latinameric-2015-44">[42]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_discrepancy-MEX"><b><a href="#ref_discrepancy-MEX">^G</a></b></span> There's a strong discrepancy between the Trends 2014,<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_109-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-109">[106]</a></sup> Trends 2015 and Trends 2016 report.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2016_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2016-172">[164]</a></sup> The cumulative capacity was revised downwards significantly for previous years in the 2016 report.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_discrepancy-ESP"><b><a href="#ref_discrepancy-ESP">^H</a></b></span> There's a discrepancy between Eur'Observ<sup id="cite_ref-pv-barometer-2014_125-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pv-barometer-2014-125">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pv-barometer-2013_126-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pv-barometer-2013-126">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pv-barometer-2012_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pv-barometer-2012-127">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pv-barometer-2011_128-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pv-barometer-2011-128">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pv-barometer-2010_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pv-barometer-2010-129">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pv-barometer-2009_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pv-barometer-2009-130">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pv-barometer-2015_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pv-barometer-2015-171">[163]</a></sup> data IEA<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2016_172-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2016-172">[164]</a></sup> data where Eur'Observer reports about 10% less installed capacity. Eur'Observ data is used here.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_discrepancy-NLD"><b><a href="#ref_discrepancy-NLD">^I</a></b></span> There's a discrepancy between Eur'Observ and IEA. Eur'Observ data used here.</dd>
</dl>
</td>
</tr>
</table>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Growth_of_photovoltaics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Projection for 2015 is an overall average of estimates from IEA, SPE (EPIA), IHS, MC, Deutsche Bank, and BNEF</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note, that the report does not mention figures for 2015 explicitly. The above figures were inferred from the published bar-chart diagram on page 14, which translate into 41.1&#160;GW for the low-case scenario and 60.2&#160;GW for the high case scenario. Figures can only be as accurate as the diagram itself. The arithmetic mean of both scenarios is 50.7&#160;GW.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See section <a href="#All_time_PV_installations_by_country">All time PV installations by country</a> for the corresponding cited sources of historical data</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See section <a href="#Forecast">forecast</a> for 2015-projections</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-140">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Displayed figures are recalculated from AC to DC-figure in megawatt-peak (MWp). The AC-to-DC conversion increases figure on average by 15% (normally in the range of 5%–30%)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Percentage figures based on "IEA-PVPS Trends 2014" report. Worldwide PV installations of 39,953 MW corresponds to 100% in the pie-chart. See table "2013—Global PV Capacity by Country in MW" for absolute figures.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">IEA-PVPS trends 2014 (see reference in footer), noted on page 33, that the country had 30 MW installed at the beginning of 2013. Cumulative of 122 MW comes from the difference in IEA-PVPS snapshot 2015 report.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Displayed data is EPIA's recalculated DC-figure in megawatt-peak (MWp). Capacity in the original data was reported in AC and amounts to 4,640 MW (cumulative) and 102 MW (installed), as per IEA-PVPS, Trends 2014, p. 34. The conversion results in a difference of approximately 15%. See cited reference.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
